\- Page 21 : According to Fanggidaej (1892) the Rote language has 14
consonant phonemes, namely /b/, /d/, /f/, /h/, /k/, /l/, /m/, /n/, /nd/,
/ng /, /ng/, /p/, /s/, and /t/. Fanggidae et al. (1998) suggested that
there are 15 consonants in the Rote language, namely /b/, /p/, /m/, /d/,
/t/, /n/, /f/, /v/, /s/, / r/, /l/, /k/, /n/, /?/ and /h/ (Fanggidae et
al, 1998:17). While Balukh (2007: xvi) states that in consonants there
are 12 consonant sounds: \[b\], \[p\], \[m\], \[f\], \[d\], \[t\],
\[n\], \[l\], \[s\], \[k\], \[n\], \[?\]; 3 semi-vowels: \[y\], \[w\],
and \[h\]; and 3 prenasal: \[mb\], \[nd\], and \[ng\]  
Some dialects do not recognize a certain sound, while other dialects
recognize that sound. For example sound prenasal \[ng\] in bonggi lahir
‘used by some dialects in western Rote, in central Rote, they use the
nasal sound \[n\] in bongi ’born’, while in eastern Rote they use the
inhibitory sound \[k\] in boki ‘born’. In addition, there are variations
of sound \[l\] and sound \[r\] used by different dialects. For example
in the words hara \[hara\] ‘voice’ and hala \[hala\] ‘voice’. Therefore,
according to the author, in Rote Grammar there are approximately 19
consonants.  
\- Page 22 : Vibrate r  
\- Page 27 : Consonant Sound \[r\] word:  
The consonant sound \[r\] is called the alveolar trembling sound. In the
distribution there are at the beginning, middle and end of the word.
Examples of the initial positions of words: ria \[riva\] ‘he’, rita
\[rita\] ‘(they) see’, rasó \[rasƆ\] ‘poison’, ratés \[rates\] ‘grave’,
and reké \[reke\] ‘count’. Examples are in the middle of the words hara
\[hara\] ‘voice’, sira \[sira\] ‘they’, feces \[feces\] ‘heavy’, and
héré \[herε\] ‘choose’. Examples at the end of words: hatahorir
\[hatahorir\] ‘people (plural)’, mésér \[meser\] ‘teacher (plural)’,
anggór \[angor\] ‘wine’, and iar \[iyar\] ‘this is all’.  
\- Page 28 : Variations of /r/ and /l/. The phoneme variation /r/ is
used in Thie, Oenale, Delha, Ringgou, Oepao, and Landu while the
variation /l/ is used in the dialects of Dengka, Lelain, Ba’a, Loleh,
Korbaffo, Bokai, Bilba, Diu, Lelenuk, Termanu, Keka , and Talae.  
\- Page 29 : Variations of /d/, /l/, and /r/. The phoneme /d/ is used in
Ba’a, Loleh, Termanu, Keka, Talae, Thie, and Korbaffo. The phoneme /l/
is used in Bokai, Bilba, Diu, Lelenuk, Dengka and Lelain. The phoneme
/r/ is used in Ringgou, Oepao, Landu, Oenale and Delha. This variation
occurs in the initial and middle positions of the word. For example, daé
‘land’ becomes laé ‘land’ and raé ‘land’.  
Variations of /r/, /l/, /n/, and /nd/. The phoneme variation /r/ is used
in Ringgou, Landu, and Oepao. Phoneme /l/ used in Bilba, Diu, Bokai, and
Lelenuk. Phoneme /n/ used in Korbaffo, Termanu, Keka, Talae. Whereas the
phoneme /nd/ is used in Ba’a, Loleh, Thie, Dengka and Oenale. This
variation occurs in phonemes that are distributed in the middle of the
word. For example, the Ringgou people call éré ‘soak’, in Bilba they
call élé ‘soak’ while in Termanu they use éné ‘soak’, and Loleh use éndé
‘soak’.  
\- Page 30 : Variations of /nd/, /l/, and /r/. The /nd/ variation is
used in Ba’a, Loleh, Thie, Termanu, Keka, Talae, Dengka, Lelain, Delha
and Oenale. Variation /l/ is used in Bilba, Diu, Lelenuk and Bokai. The
/r/ variation is used in Ringgou, Landu and Oepao. This variation occurs
in the phoneme that occupies the initial position of the word.

    ## 1 Ende     CLICS   wiki

Trill : yes  
R\_type : trill

Dataset :

Phoible : NA

Wikipedia :  
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ende_language_(Indonesia)>  
\- Trill r  
\- Approximant ɹ ⟨rh⟩

Bradley McDonnell. 2009. A Preliminary Description of Ende Phonology.
Journal of the Southeast Asian Linguistics Society II. 195-226.  
\- Page 198 : Flap/Trill ɾ/r <r>  
Approximant ɹ <rh>  
\- Page 204 : Trill/flap: The alveolar flap \[ɾ\] and the alveolar trill
\[r\] are allophones of a single phoneme that can  
occur as a single flap \[ɾ\] or multiple flaps, producing a trill \[r\].
This phoneme, represented as the flap /ɾ/ can occur in all possible
consonant positions in the word. The flap /ɾ/ contrasts with voiced and
voiceless alveolar stops and the palatal affricate.  
/ɾ/ \~ /d/ ngaru /ŋaɾu/ ‘bored’ ngadu /ŋadu/ ‘to look up’  
/ɾ/ \~ /Ɂ/ ngara /ŋaɾa/ ‘name’ ‘ata /Ɂata/ ‘person’  
/ɾ/ \~ /ɗ/ koro /koɾo/ ‘chili pepper’ kodho /koɗo/ ‘to watch’  
/ɾ/ \~ /nd/ ri’a /ɾiɁa/ ‘good’ ndia /ndia/ ‘here’  
/ɾ/ \~ /ʤ/ ngguru /ŋ ɡuɾu/ ‘ring’ ngguju /ŋɡuʤu/ ‘mumble’  
/ɾ/ \~ /n/ roko /ɾoko/ ‘to hide’ noko /noko/ ‘skinny’  
/ɾ/ \~ /s/ kera /kəɾa/ ‘to shout’ kesa /kəsa/ ‘to increase’

  - Page 205 : There are two primary approximants in Ende, the
    labiovelar approximant /w/ and the alveolar approximant /ɹ/, and one
    more restricted approximant, the lateral approximate /l/. The two
    primary approximants, the labiovelar /w/ and the alveolar /ɹ/, both
    produce a high level of turbulent airflow during articulation.
    Therefore, these approximant phonemes at first pass sound as if they
    are fricatives. In fact, the turbulent airflow present on each
    phoneme in some instances gives rise to allophonic variation between
    the approximant and the respective fricative. That is, for /w/ there
    is occasionally allophonic variation between \[w\] and \[ß\] and for
    /v/ there is occasionally allophonic variation between \[ɹ\] and
    \[z\]. The fricative allophonic variants \[ß\] and \[z\] arise when
    the level of turbulence is increased by a  
    more restricted closure between the passive and active articulators,
    which in turn causes the approximant properties to become convoluted
    or lost altogether. The environment for allophonic variation between
    approximants and fricatives is not predictable.  
    The labiovelar approximant /w/ shows contrast with the approximant
    alveolar /ɹ/ in all possible consonant positions.  
  - Page 206 : The alveolar approximant /ɹ/ is produced when the tongue
    tip is brought close to the alveolar ridge, creating turbulence in
    the airflow between the tongue tip and the alveolar ridge. As was
    noted earlier in this section, the alveolar approximant shows a high
    level of turbulence, which shows a marked contrast with say the
    English alveolar approximant /ɹ/. The increased turbulence appears
    to result from the increased level of constriction between the
    tongue tip and the alveolar ridge. The following contrasting
    segments establish the alveolar approximant /ɹ/ as a phoneme. The
    alveolar approximant /ɹ/ contrasts with all of the alveolar stops
    /d, t, nd, ɗ/.  
    \[examples\]  
    The alveolar approximant /ɹ/ contrasts with the alveolar fricative
    /s/.  
    \[examples\]  
    The alveolar approximant /ɹ/ also forms a contrast with the palatal
    affricate /ʤ/.  
    \[examples\]  
    The alveolar approximant /ɹ/ contrasts with the flap /ɾ/ in
    word-initial and intervocalic positions.  
    /ɹ/ \~ /ɾ/ rhé’é /ɹeɁe/ ‘sharp’ ré’é /reɁe/ ‘bad’  
    ngara /ŋaɾa/ ‘name’ ngarha /ŋaɹa/ ‘can’  
  - Page 207 : The lateral /l/ is far more common in Lio than in Ende.
    Cognates that occur in Lio with the lateral /l/ occur in Ende as the
    alveolar approximant /ɹ/. For instance, lawo ‘sarong’ in Lio forms a
    cognate rhawo ‘sarong’ in Ende, and the Lio numeral telu ‘three’ is
    a cognate with the Ende terhu ‘three’.5 In spite of numerous
    cognates, there is still a small subset of Ende words that contain
    /l/ that have no /ɹ/ equivalent. Therefore, the lateral approximant
    /l/ is considered a phoneme, even though it is most likely borrowed
    from Lio. The lateral /l/ contrasts with the alveolar stop and the
    palatal affricate phonemes.  
    Further, the lateral phoneme /l/ contrasts with the alveolar nasal
    /n/ and flap /ɾ/ phoneme.  
    /l/ \~ /ɾ/ gela /ɡəla/ ‘glass’ gera /ɡəɾa/ ‘to get angry’  
    Most important in establishing the lateral approximant as a phoneme
    is the contrast between the lateral /l/ and the alveolar approximant
    // in word-initial and intervocalic positions.  
    /ɹ/ \~ /l/ rho /ɹoː/ ‘body’ lo /loː/ ‘market’  
    (lala)-lé /leː/ ‘to joke’ arhé /aɹe/ ‘to fly’

Elias, Alexander (2019). Lio and the Central Flores languages
(M.A. thesis). Leiden University.  
\- Page 78 : RHOTIC ɹ r

Agob-Ende-Kawam : \[ATTENTION CE N’EST PAS LE MEME ENDE\] :  
<https://glottolog.org/resource/languoid/id/agob1244>  
Lindsey, K. L. (2021). Ende. Journal of the International Phonetic
Association, 1-21.  
\- Page 2 : Tap or flap ɾ ɽ  
\- Page 3 : Marginal: very few words begin with /R/ in Ende.  
\- Page 11 : The Ende inventory includes three liquids: an alveolar tap
/ɾ/, a retroflex flap /ɽ/, and an alveolar lateral approximant /l/. Ende
lacks the palatal lateral approximant /ʎ/ found in Idi and Taeme, which
is realized as /ɽ/ or /l/ in cognates (Evans, Lindsey & Schokkin 2019).
Unlike the other consonant categories, the liquid consonants are not
evenly distributed across phonological contexts. Among the liquids, /ɽ/
is most common word-initially, inter-vocalically, and word-finally,
although /ɾ/ approaches the count of /ɽ/ inter-vocalically. The
distribution of liquids is more similar in complex onsets and codas,
although they are far more common in onsets than in codas. The counts in
Table 2 are based on the 2017 Ende dictionary, which included
approximately 5000 words. As with the retroflex affricates, the
retroflex flap also often has weak retroflexion in its articulation. The
following figures provide a near-minimal triplet contrasting
intervocalic /ɽ/ with clear retroflexion (Figure 8), less-clear
retroflexion (Figure 9), and the alveolar tap (Figure 10).

\[rata?\] ‘hair’  
\[pira\] ‘how many’  
\- Page 64 : /r/ vs. /t/  
/d/  
/l/  
\- Page 78 : Stop + liquid  
Fricative + liquid  
\- Page 85 : \* j/\* d \> r PMP \* j and \* d became r in some

\- Page 40 : Consonant Minimal Pairs : /l/\~/r/

Michels, Marc (2017). Western Lamaholot: a cross-dialectal grammar
sketch (Master’s thesis). Leiden University.  
\- Page 20 : Liquid l, r  
Two phonemes in this table have a different phonetic value in some
dialects. Firstly the palatal glide /j/ is a postalveolar affricate
\[dZ\] in some dialects, and has in one dialect become an alveolar trill
\[r\]. Compare for example Pamakayo kayo \[kajo\] ’tree’ with its
cognate in Lewotobi kajo \[kadZoP\] (Nagaya 2011, 60). Figure 2.1 shows
the spread of these two: \[dZ\] is prevalent in Southern Lembata and
most of the Flores dialects, while \[j\] is more prominent on Solor and
Adonara. Northern Adonara is alone in having developed this phoneme into
\[r\], and the Eastern dialects a different root is used for this
concept. The spread of the forms has little to do with the dialectal
division shown earlier in Figure 2, rather it seems to have gone across
dialect boundaries at a later time, and is therefore evidence for
Lamaholot as a dialect continuum.  
The spread of y \[j\] versus j \[dZ\] and r \[r\] in kayo ’tree’
throughout the Lamaholot area. Based on the lists gathered by Keraf
(1978) and the Alorese list by Klamer (2011).  
\- Page 21 : It can be assumed that \[r\] on Northern Adonara is an
innovation, for economical reasons if nothing else. Between \[j\] and
\[dʒ\], the former has a more contiguous distribution, so an hypothesis
could be that \[j\] is an innovation which spread from the centre
outwards, leaving the original form only at the edges of the continuum.
However, upon closer inspection we can see that \[dʒ\] is the form
prevalent in all the politically and economically important coastal
settlements: Lohayong and Lamakera on Solor, Lamahala and Waiwerang on
Adonara, Lamalera and Lewoleba on Lembata, and the area around Larantuka
on Flores. In a dialect continuum innovations usually spread from
important population to the hinterlands rather than the other way
around, so it is more likely that \[j\] is the original value and \[dʒ\]
the innovation. Doyle also postulates \[j\] as the original value (Doyle
2010, 27).  
Another salient variation between dialect is the occurrence of /l/ in
the syllable onset where the next syllable starts with /r/, such as
laran ’road’ or lera ’sun’. In the dialects of Adonara and the dialects
of Flores and Solor that are placed in the same group by Grangé (2015)
this /l/ has become /r/: raran and rera.

\=\> It can be assumed that \[r\] on Northern Adonara is an innovation,
for economical reasons if nothing else : je ne suis vraiment pas sûr de
comprendre le ‘economical reasons’

\- Page 44 : Voiced apico-alveolar trill /r/ The voiced apico-alveolar
trill /r/ is the only rhotic and it appears both word-initially and
word-medially. It is in free variation everywhere between a tap \[ɾ\]
and a trill \[r\], but the tap realization is more common than the
trill:  
ra’i \[’ɾaʔi, ’raʔi\] ‘to approach’  
koro \[’kʰoɾo, ’kʰoro\] ‘chili pepper’  
The following minimal pairs show that /r/ contrasts with the other
continuants and coronal phonemes, as well as the absence of any
phoneme:  
\[examples with ø, t, d, nd, ɗ, dʒ, n, f, s, ʋ, l, ɰ in initial and
medial position\]  
\- Page 53 : Thus, the conclusions drawn from this comparison are that,
in comparable environments, the schwa /ə/ is about half as long as a
full vowel, and causes an increase of around 25% in the length of the
following consonant. It is my impression that the schwa /ə/ lengthens
different classes of consonants to different degrees, affecting
obstruents more than continuants and leaving the alveolar trill /r/
unaffected, but this must be confirmed by further phonetic analysis.  
\- Page 54 : /mra/ -\> \[məra\] ‘to live’

Tap ɾ  
\- Page 9 : In particular, /r/ and /ɾ/ merge for most speakers. In
addition, for many, especially those who are not native speakers of
Tetun Dili, there is the possibility of merging for: /v/-/b/, /ʃ/-/s/,
/ʒ/-/z/, /ɲ/-/n/, /ʎ/-/l/ and /d/-/ɾ/  
In intervocalic position /d/ is often tapped; this results in a possible
merger with /ɾ/ (and/or /r/) in some words; e.g. odamatan \[oda’matan\]
- \[oɾa’matan\] - \[oɾ’matan\] ‘door’ . - Page 10 : Some speakers
contrast trilled /r/ and tapped /ɾ/, although the distinction is not
very stable and can only be found, where it exists, in intervocalic
position in Portuguese loan words; e.g. /ka’reta/ ‘car’ and /fev’reiɾu/
‘February’ . It appears that for most speakers the two rhotics are in
free variation or in complementary distribution. For this reason, we do
not distinguish the trill and the tap in our orthography.  
/r/ is a voiced alveolar trill. A duration of 3-4 taps is common, often
reducing in faster speech. Although alternation with /r/ is common, the
trill seems the preferred rhotic in syllable-initial position at least
in careful speech ; e.g. riku \[’riku\] ‘rich’ , hamrik \[ham’ri:k\]
‘stand’.  
\- Page 11 : /ɾ/ is a voiced alveolar tap, with an optional
post-alveolar variant. In non-final position, the single tap commonly
alternates with a very short trill with 2 taps. In word-final position,
the tap is usual; e.g. fiar \[’fiaɾ\] ‘believe’.

Trill : yes

Dataset :  
CLICS, Taber, Kathleen B.; Taber, Mark H. (2015). Luang Grammar and
Phonology Sketch. SIL e‑Books, 63. Dallas: SIL International.

Kathleen B. Taber and Mark H. Taber. 2015. Luang Grammar and Phonology

\- Page 3 : Trill r  
\- Page 5 : In Luang the syllabic consonants /ᶆ ṇ r l k/ occur. They
occur in two main environments. One of these is the prefixation of verb
roots (see §2.4.2 below). The other environment is the addition of
affixes for derivational purposes. Since these syllabic consonants occur
in predictable environments they are not considered phonemic in order to
conserve phonemes. In connected speech, as opposed to isolated examples,
these word initial syllabic consonants are not syllabic, but instead
become a syllable coda in the preceding word.  
\[r\] + \[’ranә\] = \[’rranә\] /r-rana/  
/ha rrana/ \[’har ’ranә\] ‘she/he lifts’  
An epenthetic break can also occur between the homorganic phonemes /t/
and /r/ where they occur morpheme medially. It is this epenthetic break
which signals that these phonemes occur across syllable boundaries and
are not clustering together as they do elsewhere.  
\[’itə̆rә\] /it.ra/ ‘add’  
\[’putə̆rә\] /put.ra/ ‘deceive’  
\[’tremә\] /tre.ma/ ‘almost’  
\[na’trimә\] /na.tri.ma/ ‘he/she receives’

  - Page 6 : A common feature in Luang is the epenthetical schwa \[ə̆\]
    which occurs between non-homorganic consonants across syllable
    boundaries. However when non-homorganic consonant clusters occur
    syllable initial within a syllable, and if the second member of the
    cluster is the liquid /l/ or/r/ then no epenthesis occurs.
    Therefore, because its environment is predictable in that it signals
    a syllable break between two non-homorganic consonants, this
    epenthetic schwa is considered to be non-phonemic. The timing is
    also  
    significantly shorter than a normal vocoid and it is not perceived
    as being distinctive by native speakers.  
    \[’wuʔə̆ru\] /wuʔru/ ‘cooking oil’  
    \[’mԑhə̆ rә\] /mehra/ ‘sick’  
    \[’plokə̆ rә\] /p-lokra/ ‘sharp’  
    Examples syllable initial with liquids /l/ and /r/:  
    \[’treʔa\] /treʔa/ ‘left’  
    \[’prai\] /prai/ ‘k.o. drum’  
    \[’tlinә\] /tlina/ ‘ear’

  - Page 9 : /r/ \[r\] is a voiced alveolar trill. It occurs word
    initially and medially.^14  
    \[’ruri\] /ruri/ ‘thorn’  
    \[’reri\] /reri/ ‘very’  
    \[’norә\] /nora/ ‘coconut’  
    \[’kԑrnә\] /kerna/ ‘dry’  
    14\. Sometimes in fast speech /r/ will change its manner of
    articulation and be realized as a flap \[ř\] intervocalically.  
    \[ruri\].\~ \[ruři\] ‘thorn’ \[rarә\].\~.\[rařә\] ‘blood’

  - Page 12 : l/r \[’lerә\] /lera/ ‘sun’  
    \[’rerә\] /rera/ ‘post/pole’  
    \[’ralә\] /rala/ ‘they give’  
    \[’rarә\] /rara/ ‘blood’

  - Page 15 : The long consonants are interpreted to be a sequence of
    two identical phonemes or two different contiguous phonemes that
    assimilate. They are considered to be sequences rather than units
    because this interpretation economizes phonemes and still
    corresponds to acceptable syllable patterns. Not only do these
    segments occur across syllable boundaries, but most occur either as
    the result of  
    morphophonemic processes or the result of non-morphophonemic
    historical processes e.g., /wolla/ ‘moon’. These geminate segments
    are produced with a noticeable delay in their release. They occur
    both word initially and medially.  
    \[ṛ’rana\] /r-rana/ ‘they pick up’  

  - Page 17 : There are eight unambiguous consonant clusters that occur
    within the syllable patterns CCV and CCVC: /pl/, /pr/, /tl/, /tr/,
    /kl/, /kr/, /wl/ and /wr/.  

  - Page 19 : Allowable consonant clusters within a morpheme rt rk rm
    rn  

  - Page 22 : ʔau- + -rana → /ʔauryana/ ‘I pick up’  
    m(u)- + -rana → /mryana/ ‘you sg. pick up’  
    rule: /u/ + C → C/w/; /w/ → /y/ /Calv.\_\_\_

Visser, Eline (2016). A Grammar Sketch of Kalamang with a Focus on

\- Page 17 : Trill r  
\- Page 33 : Trill  
/r/  
\-\> \[r\]  
\-\> \[ɾ\] (fast speech, intervocalically)  
/r/ is an apicoalveolar trill that occurs syllable-initially and
syllable-finally. It can be uttered as a tap, which happens mainly in
fast speech and intervocalically. The linguogram in figure 3.8 shows the
contact area of tongue and palate extends quite far to the back of the
mouth, at least on one side of the tongue.  
ror ‘wood, tree’  
‘orun ’tail’  
sor ‘fish’  
\- Page 37 : /r/ - /l/ alternation is slightly more common. It is found
as stated above in the name Alif/Arif. A boy named Irul is frequently
called Ilur. The following words were also heard with alternations
between /r/ and /l/.  
sol ka’rek ∼ sor ka’rek ‘rattan’  
kor ∼ kol ‘foot’

``` 
      Minimal pairs for /r/ - /l/ can be found for each position, as can be seen in section 3.1.4.  
```

  - Page 37 : /r/ - /l/  
    initial: raŋ ‘at open sea’ - ‘lalaŋ ’hot’  
    medial: ‘orun ’animal tail’ - ‘olun ’leaf on tree’  
    final: per ‘water’ - pel ‘bunch’  

  - Page 58 : In Kalamang, this happens with the bilabial stop /p/ at
    morpheme boundaries, which weakens to a bilabial approximant /w/
    intervocalically. It also lenites after /r/ and before a vowel, such
    that:  
    p → w/{\[V\],r}+*\[V\]  
    Lenition also happens with /t/, which becomes /r/
    intervocalically:  
    t → r/ \[V\]+*\[V\]
    
    ``` 
         The following examples show lenition of /t/ to /r/. It does not matter whether /t/ is part of the root or of the affix. No examples from reduplication are known. Note that, differently  
    ```
    
    from /p/, lenition of /t/ does not happen after /r/. Thus, when the
    unanalysed suffix -te is added to marmar ‘to walk’ the result is
    marmarte ‘WALK.TE’.  
    et ‘canoe’ + -at ACC ‘erat ’canoe.ACC’  
    kaŋgeit ‘to play’ -an 1SG.POSS kaŋgeiran ‘my playing, my game’  
    et ‘canoe’ + -un 3.POSS ‘erun ’his/her/their canoe’  
    kou ‘narrow’ + -ten adjective marker ‘kouren \`narrow’  
    ‘ewa ’to speak’ + -te TE e’ware ‘speak’  

  - Page 59 : For lenition of /c/ to /j/ we have the following examples.
    Again, lenition does not happen after /r/: ter ‘tea’ + -ca 2SG.POSS
    becomes terca \`your tea’.  

  - Page 61 : Voicing assimilation is optional after /r/ for some
    sufixes, and obligatory for others. It does not happen with
    possessive -ca and -ce or the unanalysed -te. What happens when
    adjective marker -ten is added to an adjective ending in /r/ is
    unknown.

\- Page 18 : Trill r  
\- Page 19 : Batuley has two liquid phonemes: the lateral /l/ and the
trill /r/. A minimal pair illustrates their  
status as phonemes in (23).  
(23) /l/ ≠ /r/ /ˈlu/ ‘teardrop’  
/ˈru/ ‘two’  
\- Page 20 : The bilabial fricative /ɸ/ is typically realized as the
bilabial plosive \[p\] before a liquid-initial stressed syllable in a
limited set of numeral words. I present these in (26a-b). Note that
fortition is optional in the numeral ‘twenty’ where /ɸ/ precedes the
trill /r/. The choice depends upon the speaker. I have found that
fortition always occurs in the numeral ‘thirty’ where /ɸ/ precedes the
lateral /1/. Fortition may be a feature illustrating accent differences
between the Batuley villages. Further investigation is required.  
(26) a. /uruɸˈru/ \[u.ruɸ.ˈru\] \~ \[u.rup.ˈru\] ‘twenty’  
b. /uruɸˈlaes/ \[u.rup.ˈla.ɛs\] ‘thirty’  
\- Page 28 : /r/ /ˈri/ /ˈʤa.ran/ /kab.ˈra.sek/ /mar.ˈnam/ /ˈtu.bor/  
‘dugong’ ‘parrot’ ‘type of fish’ ‘type of turtle’ ‘stomach’  
\- Page 29 : Consonant clusters occur across syllable and morpheme
boundaries. There are no syllable internal consonant clusters. (43a-c)
illustrate examples of consonant clusters which occur across syllable
boundaries.  
\- Page 57 : /r/ \[r\] r

\- Page 31 : \[r\] vibrante múltiplo alveolar vozeado ocorre na posição
de ataque no início de palavra.  
\[ɾ\] vibrante simples alveolar vozeado ocorre na posição de ataque da
sílaba quando entre vogais, como segunda consoante numa sequência de
ataque CC e como coda.  
\- Page 44 : Os fones \[d\] e \[r\] são foneticamente semelhantes e
estão em contraste em ambiente idêntico (CAI)  
(14) \[ˈdɔː\] ‘feiticeiro’  
\[ˈrɔː\] ‘ essoa’  
e em ambiente análogo (CAA).  
(15) \[ˈduɾ\] ‘em urrar’  
\[ˈruː\] ‘dois’  
Logo, /d/ e /r/ são fonemas distintos.  
\-Page 48 : Os fones \[r\] e \[ɾ\] são foneticamente semelhantes e estão
em distribuição complementar (DC) como variantes posicionais do fonema
/r/. O fone \[r\] ocorre em posição de ataque da sílaba no início da
palavra, enquanto o fone \[ɾ\] ocorre em osição de ataque da  
sílaba na posição medial da palavra, em coda e como segunda consoante
numa sequência de ataque CC:  
(30) /r/ -\> \[r\] / \#\_  
\[ɾ\] / n.d.a.  
(31)  
\[r\] \[ɾ\]  
\[ˈrafu\] ‘azul’ \[ˈbɛɾo\] ‘canoa’  
\[ˈruː\] ‘dois’ \[ˈkeoɾ\] ‘vento’  
\[ˈrui\] ‘osso’ \[ˈkɾei\] ‘sentar’  
\- Page 50 : Vibrante Múltipla r  
\- Page 58 : \#*\* * \[V\] \#*\[C\]\[V\] \#\[C\]*\[V\]  
\[r\] x x  
/ɾ/ x x  
\- Page 65 : Quando ocorre o apagamento de uma consoante ou vogal, o
acento pode permanecer inalterado, ou haver uma ressilabificação com o
alongamento da vogal ou, no caso de uma apócope, o ataque se tornar
coda:  
\[’da.oɾ\] \~ \[’da.o\] ‘laranja’  
\- Page 68 : No Mambae, o apagamento consonantal pode ocorrer no ataque
no início ou meio de palavra, ou ainda na coda antes do silêncio,
lembrando-se ser este apagamento uma variação na fala dos informantes.  
\- no ataque no início da palavra:  
(84) \[’fɾɔː\] \~ \[ˈrɔː\] ‘barco’  
\- na coda no final de palavra:  
(88) \[’daoɾ\] \~ \[’dao\] ‘laranja’  
Metátese : \[ˈmoɾi\] \~ \[ˈmoɛɾ\] ‘nascer’

    ## # A tibble: 1 × 3

\- r allophones r

van den Berg, René. 1989. A Grammar of the Muna Language. Foris
Publications.  
René van den Berg. 1989. A Grammar of the Muna Language. (Verhandelingen
van het Koninklijk Instituut voor Taal-, Land- en Volkenkunde, 139.)
Dordrecht: Foris. xvi+376pp.  
\- Page 8 :  
Standard Muna r  
Tiworo r  
Siompu r  
Gumas h  
\- Page 16 : trill r  
\- Page 19 : /r/ <r> voiced apico-alveolar trill:  
rase ‘rust’  
horo ‘fly’  
\- Page 20 : /ʁ/ <gh> voiced dorso-uvular fricative:  
ghuse ‘rain’  
ragha ‘branch’  
\- Page 21 : List of contrasts:  
l - r lea ‘painful’ rea ‘blood’  
gh - r ghase ‘chin’ rase ‘rust’  
\- Page 22 : phoneme initial medial  
gh 5.6 2.2  
r 6.5 8.6  
\- Page 24 : Syllable structure in Muna is therefore as follows: (C) V  
\- Page 31 : 5. Unlike liquids do not co-occur:  
l..r.. r..l..  
Exception: lera ‘blessing’ (loan?)

Wikipedia :  
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muna_language>  
\- Trill r  
\- Fricative voiced ʁ ⟨gh⟩

van den Berg, René. 2004. Notes on the southern Muna dialect. In John
Bowden and Nikolaus Himmelmann (eds.), Papers in Austronesian
subgrouping and dialectology, 129-170. Canberra: Research School of
Pacific and Asian Studies, Australian National University.  
\- trill r  
\- South /h/ for north /r/  
The sound correspondence between /vh/ and /r/ is very conspicuous and is
one of the main indicators of the southern dialect complex. Notice the
following pairs:  
south north gloss  
bhahi bhari ‘many’  
buhi buri ‘write’  
dahu daru ‘starfruit’  
ehe ere ‘stand’  
hambi rambi ‘hit’  
hobhine robhine ‘woman’  
hoo roo ‘leaf’  
kihi kiri ‘thorn’  
sohihi soriri ‘side’  
tuhu turu ‘drop (n)’  
woha wora ‘see’  
This sound correspondence is completely regular in native vocabulary and
goes back to Proto Muna \*r. The same regular correspondence is also
found in many loan words from Indonesian/Malay, as in the following
list, with the Malay source provided in brackets following the gloss:  
south north gloss  
bhihita bhirita ‘news’ (berita)  
gaha \[ghohia\] ‘salt’ (garam)  
husa rusa ‘deer’ (rusa)  
kahadhaa karadhaa ‘work’ (kerja)  
kahatasi karatasi ‘paper’ (kertas)  
kahumbau karambau ‘buffalo’ (kerbau)  
nahakaa narakaa ‘hell’ (neraka)  
po-guhu po-guru ‘learn’ (guru)

However, there are many instances where /r/ is retained in loan words. I
n these instances there is no difference between the two dialects in
this respect.  
\[examples\]  
There are also words with Irl for which the etymology is as yet unclear,
although presumably they are loan words too, either from the northern
dialect, from Dutch (Du), or from the neighbouring language of Wolio
(WoJ).  
\[examples\]  
It is unclear why these words with /r/ have resisted the change to /h/.
A theory of lexical diffusion might suggest that these words remained
untouched by the weakening rule which was clearly still operative when
loan words entered the lexicon, though the question remains why.
Alternatively one can speculate that these words were borrowed at a time
when the sound change /r/ -\> /h/ had already completed its course.
Certainly loans from Indonesian during the last fifty years all retain
/r/: ga redha ‘church’, radio ‘radio’ etc. This last alternative seems
the more likely scenario, although there is the extra factor of
influence from Indonesian through education. The word guru ‘teacher’,
for instance, has the variant guhu, and I suspect the general use of the
Indonesian word guru may have played a role in creating the variant,
which now seems to be pushing out the older guhu. Interestingly, the
derived word po-guhu ‘learn’ does not have a variant with Irl,
presumably because that derivation does not exist in I ndonesian and
hence retained its ‘old’ pronunciation with /h/. Asimilar case is
kahadhaa ‘work’, which is sometimes pronounced as karadhaa in the south.
This explanation runs into problems with rapo-rapo ‘peanuts’, which has
a variant hapo-hapo used by the very elderly. Since there is no I
ndonesian target for this etymon, it remains unclear why there is
variation.

Glottologue :  
<https://glottolog.org/resource/languoid/id/muna1247>

Autres sources :  
<https://clics.clld.org/languages/lexirumah-stan1308>  
Recherches : r -\> 55 entries (filtered from 224 total entries)

\- Page 58 : There are several sound changes evident between the
northern and southern isolects of the Tukang Besi language, both
systematic and semi-systematic. These are both in the nature of sound
changes that have occurred in a stage ancestral to the dialect groups,
and differences in the major allophonic realisation of a phoneme, or
phonemic interpretation of a particular phonetic segment. Both of these
are discussed below with examples.

\- Page 27 : PAN \*R \> Kayeli /l/, Buru /h/  
\- Page 48 : Trill r  
The /r/ is trilled; but may become a flap or tap intervocalically  
\- Page 64 : Deletion before /r/  
Simple NP phrase heads have the genitive /-n/ deleted before words
begining with /r/  
(57) beto-n em-sia-n ‘one night’  
beto rua ‘two night’  
beto-n telo ‘three night’  
The genitive enclitic is not deleted from complex phrase heads before
words beginning with /r/.  
(59) fafu ana-n rua dii ‘those two piglets’  
Liquid /l/ is deleted before /r/.  
(60) polo ‘ten’  
po-rua ‘twenty’  
pol-telo ‘thirty’  
pol-paa ‘forty’  
\- Page 71 : Reduction of /-ro/  
Reduction of the pural enclitic /-ro/ to /-r/ also involves the loss of
a vowel, but unlike clitisation this reduction does not affect stress.
The process thus parallels the loss of paragogic /e// in syllable
strucutre. While the pluram /-ro/ functions grammatically at the phrase
level, within an NP it must occur before numerals, quantifiers and
deictics and is cliticised as a non-final element.  
(103) aso-r telo  
asu-ro  
dog-PL three  
‘three dogs’  
(104) aso-r dii  
asu-to  
dog-PL DIST  
‘those dogs’  
(105) geba-r pila  
person-PL how many  
‘how many people’  
(106) faf.mina-n fah.wanga-r paa  
pig.fat-GEN hand.digit-PL four  
‘pig-fat four fingers thick’

of more than two

    ## # A tibble: 1 × 3
    ##   Language Dataset Comments
    ##   <chr>    <chr>   <chr>   
    ## 1 Biak     CLICS   <NA>

Trill : yes  
R\_type : trill

Dataset :

Phoible :  
<https://phoible.org/languages/biak1248>  
2 inventaires  
\- PH r allophones r ɾ d  
\- GM r allophones r

Heuvel, Wilco van den. 2006. Biak: Description of an Austronesian
Language of Papua. LOT.  
\- Page 21 : Trill r (r)  
\- Page 22 : /r/ \[r\], a voiced alveolar trill. In fast speech /r/ may
be realized as the alveolar flap \[ɾ\]. It may be realized as \[d\] when
preceded by another /r/ (cf. section 2.6.3). A sequence of a /n/ and /r/
is realized as \[ndr\], while /mr/ is realized as \[mbr\].  
\- Page 23 : Consonant contrasts in word-initial position  
Alveolars and palatal: /d/-/t/-/n/-/s/-/r/-/y/  
Fricatives, trill and approximants: /f/-/s/-/r/-/w/-/y/  
Alveolars: /d/-/n/-/s/-/r/-(/t/)  
Nasals, fricatives, trill and approximants:
/m/-/n/-/v/-/f/-/s/-/r/-/w/-/y/  
\- Page 24 : Consonant contrasts in word-final position  
Alveolars: /t/-/n/-/s/-/r/ (/d/ is not attested in word-final
position)  
\- Page 25 : The other phoneme to be discussed is /l/. Neither in the
corpus, nor in the dictionaries by the Van Hasselts (1876, 1893, 1947)
and Suparno (1977) have any forms with /l/ been attested, except in some
loan words explicitly marked as such (Van Hasselt 1947:131). This makes
the status slightly different from that of /t/, which is also found in
some words that have become part of the language. Although some older
people realize /l/ as \[r\] in loanwords (like one older person who
realized kapal ‘ship’ as \[kapar\]), the great majority of speakers
realize /l/ in loan words simply as \[l\].  
The realization \[l\] is not only found in loan words, but also found as
an alternative realization of the phoneme /r/. This alternative
realization is possible in a number of words and is used for special
pragmatic effects. Although no examples were attested in the corpus of
spontaneous speech, my consultants provided expressions like the
following.  
\- Page 26 : frúr ‘make’ vs. flúr ‘strike hard’ as in wa-flúr i
‘2SG-strike.hard 3SG’ -\> ‘(you) strike him hard.’  
pram ‘strike hard’ vs. plam ‘strike hard’ as in wa-plam i
‘2SG-strike.hard 3SG’ -\> ‘youstrike him hard’  
pláf ‘blind’ as variant of práf ‘rust(y)’, as in pan pláf i ‘2SG.touch
blind 3SG’-\> ‘make him blind’  
plef ‘scrape off’ as a variant of pref ‘scrape off’, as in wa-plef i
‘2SG-srape.off 3SG’ -\> ‘scrape him off’ -\> ‘conquer him / knock him
down’  
The contexts depicted by my consultants, in which expressions like the
ones given are used, were either situations of playing (football), or
allusions to sexual intercourse (in the sense of ‘take this girl’).
Although the realization of /r/ as \[l\] instead of \[r\] brings about
some difference in meaning, this meaning difference is more of a
pragmatic nature than of a semantic nature. The difference between pram
and plam is clearly of a different nature than the difference between,
for example, the difference between sam ‘hot’ and sar ‘sharp’.  
\- Page 38 : Liquid-or r-initial  
/rm/  
/rw/  
\- Page 39 : The origin of the prefix r- is not clear.  
\- Page 40 : Now consider the sequences not obeying the SSP. For some of
them, the cluster must phonetically be part of two syllables. This is
true for clusters in which an initial /r/ or an initial nasal is
(phonetically) followed by a stop. In these cases, when the words are
uttered in isolation, the initial /r/ or nasal form a syllable on their
own. Whereas /n/ and /r/ survive as ‘phonetic syllables’ at the surface,
there is no reason to assume that the language structurally allows for
syllables consisting of a nasal or r only.2  
2\. If we stated that the language phonemically has syllables containing
of a nasal only, nothing would be won. It does not account for the
remarkable set of initial clusters, because we are still left with many
non-nasal initial clusters that ask for an explanation. Second, in the
analysis of final clusters, it is clear that nasal-final and /r/-final
clusters behave exactly the same as other clusters. Just like other
clusters, they are broken up by epenthesis, as discussed in 2.2.1 and
2.2.3. If final nasals and final /r/ phonemically formed a syllable on
their own, there would be no explanation for the epenthesis of \[e\].  
\- Page 42 : r-final  
/pr/ d-opr ‘3SG-jump’  
/kr/ i-mkikr 3SG-shiver’  
/fr/ s<y>ofr ‘\<3SG\>gather’  
/sr/ p<y>esr ‘\<3SG\>defecate’  
/vr/ k<y>avr ‘\<3SG\>return  
/wr/ r<y>owr’\<3SG\>hear’  
\- Page 54 : In monomorphemic words, the sequence /n/ + /r/ is not
attested. In polymorphemic words, a sequence of /n/ and /r/ is realized
as \[ndr\], as was mentioned in 2.1.1. The sequence /m/ + /r/ is found
in both polymorphemic and monomorphemic words, and realized as \[mbr\].

``` 
          (18) i-mráne [imbráne]  
          3SG-walk  
          'he walks'  
    The reduplication pattern clearly shows that b is not lexical. The reduplicated variant of mrán is marandán, a form that follows the regular pattern described in section 7.3.3. Both the intrusion of b in mr and the intrusion of d in nr are the result of variation in timing between the different dimensions or gestures required for the realization of the individual segments (Booij 1995:137, Browman and Goldstein (1990)). Nasals are produced with an open velic passage, so that the air stream escapes through the nasal cavity, while all other segments are produced with a closed velic passage. The b and d- intrusion described just above are the result of denasalization (or closing of the velic passage) while the air stream through the oral cavity is still obstructed. The place features of the intrusive stops are those of the preceding nasal.  
    With respect to the realization of /r/ after /n/, a difference can be observed between words belonging to major lexical classes (verbs and nouns) on the one hand and other words and other lexical items on the other hand. For major lexical classes, a sequence of /n/ and /r/ is realized as [ndr]. The phoneme /r/, in other words, ' survives' at the surface structure. For other words and morphemes, however, /r/ is virtually always realized as [d] when following /n/. Thus, the preposition ro 'LOC' is realized as [do] in i-mrán ro di-ne ' 3SG-walk LOC place-this' [imrándodine], while for the r-initial directionals that are part of motion adverbs, the realization [d] following /n/ is obligatory (as in van-rum 'side-inside' [vandum], cf. section 9.5.4, table 10). As will be shown in 2.6.3, a similar distinction between words of major lexical classes and other lexical items holds for the sequence of /r /and /r/.  
```

  - Page 56 : 2.6.3 Sequence of /r/ and /r/  
    /r/,/d/ and /n/ differ minimally. Although they have been shown to
    be different phonemes in 2.1.1 above, dialectical variation shows
    that they are very close. Compare the following examples:  
    (25) rambav ‘<type of mammal>’  
    dambav ‘<type of mammal>’  
    nambav ‘<type of mammal>’  
    As geminates are not attested in monomorphemic words, a sequence of
    /r/ + /r/ also is not found. As for other contexts, a distinction
    should be made between words belonging to the major lexical
    categories of verbs and nouns on the one hand, and other lexical
    items on the other hand. For verbs and nouns, /r/ preceded by /r/ in
    the same intonational phrase is optionally realized as \[d\]. Thus,
    the compound mnivr rum has the following two realizations:  
    (26) mnivr rum  
    bee nest  
    \[mnivr:um\], \[mnivrdum\]  
    ‘bee nest’ \[FFbc\], \[FPbi\]  
    Lexical items that do not belong to the major lexical classes, on
    the other hand, show a very strong tendency to be realized as \[d\]
    following /r/, as in sufrar rao ‘3DU-run until’, where rao is
    realized as \[da(w)o\].

Steinhauer, Hein. 2006. Biak. In Adelaar, Alexander and Himmelman,
Niklaus (eds.) Austronesian Languages of Asia and Madagascar 793–795.
1st edn. Routledge.  
\- Page 754 : Trill r  
\- Page 795 : /n/ before a velar or labial stop is optionally
assimilated as \[ŋ\] and \[m\] respectively. The sequence /mr/ is always
realized as \[mbr\].  
The sonorants (/m, n, r/) may become syllabic through the loss of
preceding /e/:  
d-oper (3s.SBJ-jump) ‘he/she/it jumps’  
d-opr ro war ve-ki \[dhp Srowár eki\] (3s.SBJ-jump LOC water REL-flow)  
‘he/she/it jumps into the river’  
d-opr ve (a)ya \[dhpSr e(a)yá\] (3s.SBJ-jump DIR 1s)  
‘he/she/it jumps towards me’  
\- Page 804 : After r, y or a nasal -ri- is replaced by -di-. The exact
phonetic conditions for the variation should be further investigated.

\- Obstruents Fricative ɣ\~ʁ  
\- Commonly transcribed /r/. This is usually pronounced as a uvular
fricative or approximant \[ʁ\] or velar fricative \[ɣ\], and sometimes
as a uvular \[ʀ\] or alveolar trill \[r\] or alveolar flap \[ɾ\],
depending on the background of the speaker.

<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hebrew_language>  
\- Most Israelis today also merge /ʕ ħ/ with /ʔ χ/, do not have
contrastive gemination, and pronounce /r/ as a uvular fricative \[ʁ\] or
a voiced velar fricative \[ɣ\] rather than an alveolar trill, because of
Ashkenazi Hebrew influences.

\- Page 50 : \[l\] and \[r̃\] \[l\] is a voiced laterraly realeased

\[r̃\]us a voiced liquid with a flapped or slightlt trilled release.

and are members of the classes of allophones comprisong the phonemes /l/
and /r/.  
The following phones are established: /r/  
\- Page 53 : Flap Liquid r  
The liquids, /l/ and /r/, articulated at the alveolar ridge, contrast
only as to method of release, the one, /l/, being a lateral and the
other, /r/, a flap of the tongue tip, or a slight trill.

4 inventaires

Schachter, Paul and Otanes, Fé T. 1972. Tagalog Reference Grammar.
Berkeley: University of California Press. (Reprinted in 1983).  
\- Page 24 : Tap or Trill  
The most common allophone of Tagalog /r/ is the alveolar tap, \[r̊\]
(=\> c’est un point dessus): a single rapid tap of the tip of the tongue
against the alveolar ridge. This sound is similar to a very rapidly
released English \[d\], and is the sound used by most Americans as an
allophone of /d/ in a word like ‘shudder’, and as an allophone of /t/ in
a word like shutter. The single tap is sometimes replaced by the
alveolar trill, \[r\], a series of rapid taps, especially in emphatic
speech.  
\[examples\]  
\- Page 25 : It is likely that \[r̊\] and \[d\] were once allophones of
a single Tagalog phoneme. While there is no doubt that the two sounds
now belong to different phonemes (witness the constrast between such
pairs of words as maramdamin /maeamdamin/ ‘sensitive’ and madamdamin
/madamdamin/ ‘moving’), the earlier status of the sounds is reflected in
the existence of a great deal of alternation between the phonemes /r/
and /d/. This alternation can best be understood on the assumption that
/d/ is the basic phoneme in all cases where there is alternation, and
that it is replaced by /r/ under certain specifiable conditions.  
\[Plus d’explications sur le replacement\]

Bloomfield, Leonard. 1917. Tagalog Texts With Grammatical Analysis.
(University of Illinois Studies in Language and Literature, 3.) Urbana,
Illinois: University of Illinois.  
Version numérisée +
<https://www.gutenberg.org/files/50797/50797-h/50797-h.htm#ch2.a>  
\- Page 134 : Trill (voiced) r  
\- Page 136 : 17. Final d in word-formation is always replaced by r
before the vowel of a suffix: lákad walk, lakar-àn walking-party.  
Initial d becomes r in the sentence frequently after the final vowel of
a preceding word and occasionally even after a final non-syllabic, in
the words daàn hundred (not in daàn road), damò, dàw, dìn, díne, díto,
diyàn, doòn; e. g. ako rìn I too.  
In word-formation some words frequently change d to r after the vowel of
a prefix or a reduplication, and others do not: pa-raàn means (daàn
road, way), but i-pa-dalà be sent (dalà bring, carry). The words (or
roots) that have changeable d- are those mentioned in the preceding
paragraph and daàn, -dáka, damdàm, dámi, dámot, daŋàl, dapàʾ, dápat,
dáti, -dátig, -datìŋ, dikìt, -dinìg, -díwaŋ, -dúkit, -dumè, dúnoŋ,
dúsa.  
\- Page 137 : 21. r is the voiced tongue-tip trill, postdental. It
occurs between vowels within simple, underived words: áraw sun, day,—no
30doubt as a substitute for d, which never occurs in this position;
everywhere else r is merely a variant of d (§ 17).

Himmelman, Niklaus. 2006. Tagalog. In Adelaar, Alexander and Himmelman,
Niklaus (eds.) The Austronesian Languages of Asia and Madagascar
350–356. 1st edn. Routledge.  
\- Page 352 : Tap or trill r  
\- Page 354 : Furthermore, /d/ often becomes /r/ in intervocalic
position, although this is by no means always the case. Compare doón
‘there’ with maroón ‘be there’, tawad ‘discount’ with tawaran ‘ask for
a discount on sth’, etc. Note that /r/ does not occur in initial
position or after a consonant in Tagalog native words.

Wikipedia :  
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tagalog_language>  
\- Rhotic ɾ ⟨r⟩  
\- /ɾ/ and /d/ were once allophones, and they still vary grammatically,
with initial /d/ becoming intervocalic /ɾ/ in many words.  
\- The /ɾ/ phoneme is an alveolar rhotic that has a free variation
between a trill, a flap and an approximant (\[r<sub>ɾ</sub>ɹ\]).

Tagalog (2005). Keith Brown (ed.). Encyclopedia of Language and
Linguistics (2 ed.). Elsevier.

Glottologue :  
<https://glottolog.org/resource/languoid/id/taga1270>

<https://clics.clld.org/languages/diacl-29200>

\- Page 382 : In syllable-initial position /p j r/ occur in a small
number of foreign (mainly French) loans, e.g. \[pan\] \< panne
‘breakdown’, \[ɣara\] \< garage,\[bija\] \< billiard. For many
speakers, however, /p/ is realized as \[b/ɓ\] and /r/ as \[z\].

Wikipedia :  
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vietnamese_language>  
\- Rhotic r \[r\]  
\- r (\[z\] in the Northern dialect, \[r\] in the Central and Southern
dialects)  
\- There is also a merger of r and g in certain rural varieties in the
South

<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vietnamese_phonology>  
\- In southern speech, the phoneme /ɺ/, generally represented in
Vietnamese linguistics by the letter ⟨r⟩, has a number of variant
pronunciations that depend on the speaker. More than one pronunciation
may even be found within a single speaker. It may occur as a retroflex
fricative \[ʐ\], an alveolar lateral flap \[ɺ\] (unrounded), a flap
\[ɾ\] or a trill \[r\], especially in loanwords. Some rural speakers
from Mekong Delta pronounced /ɺ/ as \[ɣ\] or \[j\], but this is not
considered formal.

Glottologue :  
<https://glottolog.org/resource/languoid/id/viet1252>

Autres sources :  
<https://clics.clld.org/languages/wold-Vietnamese>

\- r̺ pas d’allophones; ɹ̺ pas d’allophones

Donohue, Mark. n.d. Wulguru: A salvage study of the north-eastern
Australian language from Townsville. (Languages of the World/Materials.)
Lincom Europa.

Wikipedia :  
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wulguru_language>  
\- Trill r  
\- Approximant ɹ

Donahue, Mark (2007). Wulguru: a salvage study of a north-eastern
Australian language from Townsville. Munich: LINCOM.  
\- Page 5 : Rhotics rr rh (r)  
The digraph <rr> represent a trilled rhotic, and <rh> represents an
approximate. The plain <r> on its own shows that I could not induce the
manner of the articulation from the records left, and that the work done
on the language in the early 1970s by Tsunoda and Sutton did not uncover
the word in question, and so the manner of articulation remains a
mystery. Where possible, I have included, in the lexicon, any possible
cognates with languages spoken in the vicinity of Wulguru as possible
clues as to how they were articulated. Thus, for instance, the entry
guridhala ‘eaglehawk’ is followed by the note that in Biri, a language
spoken to the south and east of Wulguru, that was used as a kind of
lingua franca by the aborigines of the area after the initial European
settling, the word for eaglehawk is gurridhala, with a trill. It thus
may be reasonable to assume that the rhotic in the Wulgurugaba word is
also a trill.  
The other realisation of this orthographically indeterminate <r> is that
following a vowel, it  
may indicate that the vowel, especially an /a/, is lengthened– that is,
phonetically \[a:\], not \[ar\] or \[aɹ\]. In the word for ‘Great
Barrier Reef’, Price has transcribed it as both “Moor’gar” and as
“Moo’ga”, suggesting that the actual pronunciation of the word might
have be \[mu:ga\]. For ‘cook’, Price gives ‘agah’ for the verb without
inflection, and ‘agargu’ for the purposive form of the verb. In these
two examples from the same lexical item the length is indicated in two
different ways, once with an ‘r’ and once without, with an ‘h’. It seems
likely, therefore, that the actual forms were \[aga:\] and \[aga:gu\],
and that the orthographic ‘r’ should not be taken to represent a rhotic
phoneme. As a possible allophonic note to this, we should observe that
the /u/ vowel seems to be lower than the English /u/ in most
environments, indicated by the frequent transcription with ‘o’, ‘oh’ or
‘or’. The fact that, in words such as ‘Great Barrier Reef’ cited
above, Price occasionally transcribed the sound with ‘oo’ and not ‘o’,
suggests that length might have preserved the more extreme quality of
the vowel. I have marked this vowel length with a doubling of the symbol
for the vowel; ‘Great Barrier Reef’ would thus be written as mugaa.  
\- Page 8 : C1: rh (=\> pas de rr)  
\- Page 9 : For instance, looking at the figures of occurrence of the
/r/ phoneme, in C1, C2, C3 and C4 positions the /r/ occurs to a fairly
proportionate degree, roughly the same as other semivowel or liquid
phonemes, although there are rather a lot of /r/s in C3 position
relative to the others. In C5 position, however, the position denoting a
single consonant in the middle of a word, /r/ occupies 25.6% of the
total; the inference that may be made from this is that the strong
suspicion that there were two rhotics in the language is confirmed – in
the other C position, only one or other of the rhotics could occur,  
and so there was not a disproportionate weighting towards the rhotic. In
C5 position, on the other hand, both /rr/ and /rh/ may occur, and the
under-differentiation of rhotics by (especially) Price is the reason
that there is a skew towards rhotics here and not in the other
positions.

<https://glottolog.org/resource/languoid/id/wulg1239>

agargu -\> akaɹku  
wirragu -\> wiraku

Blake, Barry J. (2003). The Warrnambool language: a consolidated account

\- Page xi : Retroflexes are represented by digraphs beginning with r:

\- Page 22 : rr\* r̠\*  
\*The rhotics are not distringuished in the old sources, so they have
all been transcribed as r.  
\- Page 24 : There would almost certainly have been two rhotics, a flap
or trill and a glide, but since no source makes such a distinction, I
have transcribed all rhotics as r. In quoting words from languages where
a distinction between two rhotics is attested rr is used for the
flap/trill and r̠ for the glide.  
\- Page 25 : Initial: - Intervocalic: parayt Final: maar  
girl man  
It seems that syllables could have codas consisting of a r or l
(liquids) followed by p, k, m or ng (non-coronal oral or nasal stops)  
\- Page 26 : From a consideration of cognates in other languages it
seems that both the flap/trill (rr) and the glide (r) occurred in codas,
but for many words is no clue.  
rp

rng

clusters of a stop plus r. \[example with text\]

\- Page 1 : Flap ř  
Non-sllabic Vocoids r  
\- Page 2 : /t/ contrasts with /ř/ as in ’putippae they two and
’puřippae they plural, tinku’purta catflish and pur’řak continuing,
’taelippae child and ’řaeppura  
/ř/ and /l/ contast as in ’pařa buttocks and pala roof, ’kurřo here
and an’korlo dry, bald. They constrast with /r/ as in ’pořita upland
grass ’porittal rice grass, ’tarpař cypress pine and ’watpar type of
oak, ’nolkkiya you tease and norkkiya you do habitually, ’lama showel
spear head and ’raman fluff; ’tařakkařa lift it up\!, ’talakkař road,
and tarana sand  
\- Page 3 : THe non-syllabic vocoids /y,w,r/ contrast as follows:  
\[exemples, pas vraiment des paires minimales\]

``` 
        The alveolar consonants /t, n, ř, l/ have retroflex variants. [ř.] occurs word medially following /r/ as in /'narřappa/ ['narř.pa] alone.  
        /ř/ has a trilled variant which tends to occu word finally and in consonant clusters except following /r/ as in /yoř/ [yor̃] rain, /'yaempae/ ['yar̃mpae] husband, wife's brother,   /'tarařa / ['tanařa] or shortened form [tanr̃a] rock.  
        Word median and final [rt., rn., rř., rl] are interpreted as /r/ plus alveolar consonants, that is as /rt, rn, rř, rl/. This interpretation is based on the phonetic [r] quality whihc is heard between the vowel and the follwoing retroflexed contoid, the occurrence of /r/ as the first member of consonant clusters with all points of articulation except the alveolar, the alveolar, the absence of contrast word initially between retroflexion and non-retroflexion and the economy of four less consonants which results.  
        /rt/ contrasts with /t/ as in 'wartuna dog and 'wata young one; 'mam  
        /rn/ contrasts with /n/ as in 'marnna sun and 'manna jungle; an'kurn his subsection adn an'kunkun eavy  
        /rř/ contrasts with /ř/ as in pur'řatti bird and pi'řatti rice; 'marřa tail and 'mařa comparative  
        /rl/ contrasts with /l/ as in 'marlan type fish and 'kalan hook, tarl hasten! and tal desire  
```

  - Page 7 : All single consonants occurinitially, medially, and
    finally.

Green, Rebecca. 1987. A Sketch Grammar of Burarra. (MA thesis).  
\- Page 3 : Rhotics /r/ rr /r./ r  
the alveolar rhotic is a trill, and the retroflex one a continuant.

{rr(C)(V)\_\_\_}

Anderson, Ralph Dewitt. 1963. A Grammar of Laz. (Doctoral dissertation,
University of Texas).  
<https://www.proquest.com/openview/8ae3e4ece3299bc9afd5500c112f2898/1>  
\- Page 10 : Semivowel r  
Resonants l

Kojima, Gôichi and Bucakliši, Ismail Avcı. 2003. Lazca Gramer / Lazuri
Grameri / Laz Grammar. Istanbul: Chiviyazıları. 438pp. -\> Pas trouvé

Lacroix, René. 2009. Description du dialecte laze d’Arhavi (caucasique
du sud, Turquie): Grammaire et textes. (Doctoral dissertation, Lyon:
Université Lumière Lyon 2; 927pp.)  
\- Page 10 : Selon les locuteurs, la chute de /r/ (► 3.2.3) peut être
plus ou moins marquée.  
\- Page 21 : approximant r  
\- Page 23 : /r/ ruba « tranchée » rak’ani « colline » Rizeni « Rize »  
\- Page 27 : Occlusive + Liquide  
br : brits’ey « déchiré », bresti « gris (couleur) »  
t’r : t’rap’ums « il fait du bruit avec sa bouche en mangeant »  
Liquide + Occlusive  
rd : rdei « élevé (en parlant d’un enfant) »  
\- Page 30 : Groupe consonantique final \[examples\]  
\- Page 31 : La rencontre de voyelles résulte en général de la chute
d’une consonne, la plupart du temps /r/ (► 3.2.3)  
Dans les mots d’origine laze, les groupes de voyelles à l’intérieur d’un
morphème ne sont pas primaires ; ils résultent de la chute de /r/. Il
faut noter toutefois que certains locuteurs n’acceptent pas la
réinsertion de /r/.  
\- Page 33 : Les groupes de quatre voyelles que j’ai relevés résultent
de la chute de /r/ et de la présence du clitique de discours rapporté
-a.  
\- Page 56 : En débit rapide, les séquences de voyelles /ae/ et /oe/ ont
tendance à se simplifier par assimilation progressive en /aa/ et /oo/
respectivement. Les séquences de voyelles résultent la plupart du temps
de la chute de /r/ (► 3.2.3) :  
bore \> boe \> boo « je suis »  
varen \> vaen \> vaan « il n’est pas »  
\-are \> -ae \> -aa « désinence du futur, Série I, 2e personne du
singulier »  
\- Page 61 : Alternance libre /r/ \~ /y/ \~ ∅  
On trouve de nombreux mots ou morphèmes dans lesquels alternent
librement /r/, /y/ et zéro. La liste ci-dessous en donne quelques
exemples.  
\[examples\]  
oxori \~ oxoyi \~ oxoi « maison »  
\[examples\]  
Dans quelques cas, la variante en /y/ n’est pas attestée. Les variantes
/y/ et ∅ semblent plus rares devant consonne, et non attestées en début
de mot :  
\[examples\]  
r’akani  
\[examples\]  
\- Page 62 : L’alternance /r/ \~ ∅ résulte de deux facteurs :
l’amuïssement de /r/ et, inversement, l’insertion d’un /r/
épenthétique. L’étymologie permet de décider lequel de ces deux
processus est en jeu. Dans les cas ci-dessous, par exemple, le /r/ est
d’origine :  
zori \~ zoyi \~ zoi « fortement » turc zor  
\[examples\]  
Au contraire, dans les exemples ci-dessous, l’alternance résulte de
l’insertion d’un /r/ épenthétique :  
padişai \~ padişari « sultan » turc padişah  
k’abaeti \~ k’abareti « faute » turc kabahat  
epei \~ eperi « assez de, pas mal de » turc epey  
\- Page 63 : Il semble que dans la plupart des cas, l’alternance /r/ \~
∅ résulte de l’amuïssement de /r/.  
Les variantes en /y/ de la liste en (36) peuvent s’expliquer soit par la
confusion des phonèmes /r/ et /y/, soit par une réfection fautive des
mots dans lesquels /r/ a disparu. Dans la liste en (36), j’ai
volontairement présenté les données d’une façon « non orientée », ce qui
permet de ne pas préjuger de l’origine de /r/ et /y/, en l’absence de
données  
étymologiques pour tous les mots.  
L’application des processus présentés jusqu’ici aboutit à donner à un
certain nombre de mots ou morphèmes jusqu’à six formes différentes,
toutes en variation libre : 1; 2; 3; 1 et 3; 4; 1; 3 et 4  
1\. confusion de /r/ et /y/  
2\. chute de /r/  
3\. chute de la voyelle finale  
4\. préfixation de /n/  
Les formes terminées par /y/ (gyay, ngyay, sey, -ey et oxoy) pourraient
également  
s’analyser comme résultant de la chute de /r/ suivie de la
désyllabification de /i/.  
\- Page 64 : Les séquences /rn/ et /nn/  
La séquence /rn/ est parfois réalisée /nn/ par assimilation régressive,
ce qui donne lieu  
aux variantes suivantes :  
jurneçi \~ junneçi « quarante »  
ğurni \~ ğunni « conduit (nom) »  
ar-na \~ an-na « un-SUB »  
maşkurnu \~ maşkunnu « j’ai eu peur »  
La séquence /rn/ peut se réaliser /l/ dans les verbes de la Classe 1b (►
9.9.2) :  
\[komodumelan\] comparer avec : \[moydumernan\]  
|ko-m-o-du-mer-nan| |mo-i-du-mer-nan|  
« ils lui mettent qch » « ils se mettent qch »  
\- Page 65 : Inversement, la séquence /nn/ est parfois dissimilée en
/rn/ : cerneti « paradis » \< turc cennet. Ce phénomène se produit en
particulier avec les verbes de la Classe 1c (► 9.9.3), lorsque la racine
est suivie de l’indice personnel -nan :  
\[examples\]  
La séquence /nn/ peut aussi être simplifiée en /n/ :  
\[examples\]  
\- Page 119 : Dans les dérivés turk-uli « turc » et arkab-uli « habitant
d’Arhavi », le suffixe -uri est dissimilé en -uli sous l’influence du
/r/ de la base. On remarque que la dissimilation n’est pas systématique
(cf. amerik’uri, arkaburi).  
\- Page 387 : Dans un troisième temps, les séquences /sr/ (dans \* -a-s
ren) et /tr/ (dans \*-a-t ren) se sont simplifiées par élimination de
/r/ :  
\[examples\]  
Ceci indique qu’à ce stade, l’auxiliaire et le verbe lexical ne
formaient plus deux mots indépendants. En effet, dans les constructions
où le verbe « être » est précédé d’un mot terminé par /s/ ou /t/, /r/ se
maintient :  
\- Page 588 : Le suffixe -mer peut avoir la forme -me, en raison de la
chute fréquente de /r/ (► 3.2.3).

par un Laze du village de Yolgeçen, plus âgé que mes autres
informateurs. On constate qu’il conserve bien les /r/ (incirare,
bincirae).

Trill : no  
R\_type : no trill

Dataset :

Phoible :  
<https://phoible.org/inventories/view/2090>  
\- ɾ pas d’allophones

Seki, Lucy. 2000. Gramática do kamaiurá. Editora da Universidade
Estadual de Campinas.  
\- Page 409 : Sonorantes Vibrante (tap) r  
\- Page 412 : Vibrante  
A vibrante alveolar simples /r/ não ocorre seguindo ou precedendo
pausa. Realizase como tap \[ɾ\] em ambiente oral. Em ambiente nasal,
realiza-se como \[ɾ̃\] (neste trabalho vem transcrito como \[r\]):  
/kururu/ \[kuru’ru\] “cururu (sapo)”  
/i’irũ/ \[ĩĩr̃ũ\] “marido dela”  
\- Page 414 : Contrastes  
/t/ e /r/  
/n/ e /r/  
/r/ e /ts/  
\- Page 420 : Distribuição dos fonemas nas sílabas  
/r/ Inicio de silaba : Inicial - Medial + | Fina de silaba : Inicial,
Medial - Final -  
\- Page 425 : alterna morfologicamente com o prefixo (r-} “relacional”  
\- Page 428 : Alternâncias entre fonemas  
Os fonemas /t/ e /p/ finais de raízes ou radicais alternam com os
fonemas /r/ e /w/, respectivamente, ao precederem vogal na fronteira de
morfema.  
/jawat/ “onça”  
/jawar-a rehe/ “por causa da onça”  
/oket/ “ele dorme”  
/n=oker-ite/ “ele não dorme”  
A alternância ocorre também na fronteira de palavras, caso em que, além
de radicais verbais, abrange itens de outras classes lexicais, como
advérbios (numerais) e certas partículas terminados em /t/ ou /p/:  
/ejot/ “venha\!”  
/ejor=ik/ “quero que venhas”  
/mo’apit/ “três”  
/mo’apir \# aket/ “dormi três (dias)”  
\- Page 429 : Como colocado anteriormente, o fonema /r/ ocorre somente
em posição medial de enunciado, e o fonema /t/ ocorre não só em posição
mediai, mas também seguindo ou precedendo pausa. Isso sugere a
possibilidade de se considerar que o \[t\] final resulta do
ensurdecimento de \[r\].  
Considerando-se que os processos são paralelos, adota-se aqui a
interpretação de alternância entre fonemas- /t/:/r/

Wikipedia :  
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kamayur%C3%A1_language>  
\- Flap ɾ  
\- Sonorants Trills r (=\> mauvaise traduction de ‘vibrantes’)

3 inventaires  
\- UPSID ᴅ pas d’allophones  
\- AA r allophones r  
\- GM r allophones r

Bird, Charles and Hutchinson, J. and Kante, Mamadou. 1977. An Kan
Bamanakan Kalan: Beginning Bambara. Bloomington: Indiana University
Linguistics Club.  
\- Page 3 : There are no true equivalents to the Bambara r in English or
French. The English /r/ is generally a liquid sound, and the French In
is a velar r prounounced in the back of the mouth. The Bambara In on the
other hand is a tapped sound that may sometimes sound like a /d/ to the
ear. It is quite similar to the In found in Spanish. The Bambara In is
pronounced by tapping the tip of the tongue against the ridged area of
the roof of the mouth, right behind the front teeth. In occurs
infrequently at the beginning of words.  
\[les mêmes exemples que en dessous\]

Hartell, Rhonda L. (ed.) 1993. Alphabets des langues africaines. UNESCO
and Société Internationale de Linguistique.  
\- Page : 188 : /r/ <r>  
Ministère de l’éducation nationale. 1982. Règles d’orthographe des
langues nationales. Bamako: DNAFLA

Chanard, C. 2006. Systèmes Alphabétiques Des Langues Africaines. Online:
urlhttp://sumale.vjf.cnrs.fr/phono/.

Konatè, Adama and Vydrine, Valentin. 1989. Le système phonologique du
dialecte bamana du Bèlèdugu. Mandenkan 17. 1–40.  
\- Page 39 : /t/ et /d/ s’opposent aux autres dentales /r/, /l/, /n/ \!
koto “partie basse du dos” : godo “exciter” :kolo “os” : koro “petite
calebasse pour boire la biere de mil”  
Les phonèmes /l/ et /r/ en position intervocalisque sont parmi les plus
fréquents. Ils sont compatibles avec presque toutes les combinaisons de
voyelles lorsque la voyelle précédente n’est pas nasale, c’est-à-àdre
dans les mots de type CVCV, CV:CV, CVCV. Si la voyelle de la première
syllabe est nasale, alors l’opposition des /l/:/r/:/n/ intervocalique se
neutralise et \[n\] est alors le représentant de l’archiphonème.

Wikipedia :  
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bambara_language>  
\- R – ɛr – \[r\]  
\- ߙ/ߚ – ra – \[r\]  
\- Trill r

Glottologue :  
<https://glottolog.org/resource/languoid/id/bamb1269>

Autres sources :  
<https://clics.clld.org/languages/hantganbangime-Bambara>  
Recherches : r -\> 34 entries (filtered from 209 total entries)

<http://www.mali-pense.net/akbk1-cours-01.html> (avec audios)  
Il n’y a pas de véritable équivalent du /r/ bambara en français ni en
anglais. Le /r/ anglais est en général une consonne liquide, et le /r/
français est une consonne vélaire, prononcée à l’arrière du palais. En
revanche le /r/ bambara est un son battu qu’on peut parfois confondre à
l’oreille avec un /d/. Il est assez similaire au /r/ espagnol. Le /r/
bambara se prononce en tapant l’extrémité de la langue sur la voute
palatale juste derrière les dents de devant (“consonne battue alvéolaire
voisée”).  
On le trouve rarement au début des mots, il est le plus souvent
prononcé, voire écrit, avec une voyelle le précédent : le prénom Rokia
se prononce oRokia, le mot “radio” s’écrit “arajo”  
Bambara (ex.) sens  
rɔ “dans, à” \[=\> j’ai l’impression que dans l’audio il est trillé\]  
Ramata (Aramata) “nom féminin”  
bara “récipient”  
bere “bâton”  
bɛrɛ “assez” biran “beaux-parents”  
bɔrɛ “sac”  
buru “trompette”

Trill : no  
R\_type : no trill

Dataset :  
CLICS, wiki

Phoible : NA

Wikipedia :  
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tommo_So>  
\- Tap ɾ

Laura McPherson. 2013. A Grammar of Tommo So. (Mouton Grammar Library,
62.) Berlin, Boston: Berlin: De Gruyter Mouton. xxvi+622pp.  
\- Page 8 : Unlike some Dogon languages, /l/ and /r/ are contrastive,
but neither liquid occurs in word-initial position. Only sonorant
consonants can serve as codas.  
The transcription system used in this grammar is a modified form of IPA.
The divergences are intended to make it closer to standard orthographies
in use for other West African languages like Bambara. Thus, affricate
/ʥ/ is written ‘j’, tap /ɾ/ as ‘r’, and /j/ as ‘y’, while long vowels
are written as two consecutive vowels (‘aa’ rather than /aː/).  
\- Page 16 : Tap r \[ɾ\]  
\- Page 17 : geminate \[rr\] is not attested (in native words)  
\- Page 25 : Tommo So /r/ (practical orthography) is pronounced as an
alveolar tap (IPA \[ɾ\]). This pronunciation is remarkably consistent;
it has no other rhotic allophones. However, due to a process of rhotic
dissimilation, /r/ may alternate with \[l\]. See section 3.7.6 for
further discussion.  
The following minimal pairs show that continuancy, nasality, and
rhoticity are contrastive for /r/. Since liquids cannot appear
word-initially, /r/ is only contrastive in non-initial positions.  
Minimal pairs for /r/ vs…  
/d/ jàrá ‘knock down’ jàdá ‘to calculate’  
/n/ gìrɛ́ ‘watch over’ gìnɛ́ ‘house’  
/l/ sárá ‘pay’ sálá ‘bad’  
\- Page 39 : In word-initial position, the liquids /r/ and /l/ are not
allowed. This restriction is stronger for /r/ in that loanwords with
initial /r/ tend to be repaired by epenthesis, which is not true for
/l/. Unlike in final position, however, the epenthetic vowel in initial
position is a copy of the following vowel, as in:  
(56) a. Ramata (female name) → aràmátá  
b. réunion ‘meeting’ → ɛrɛ́ núyón  
For more on vowel epenthesis, see section 3.4.6. Only one /r/-initial
word is left unrepaired, and that is the ideophone rék, an intensifier
for the number ‘one’.  
\- Page 41 : Word-final restrictions  
/r/ is usually repaired with epenthesis, possibly due to the obligatory
release of the tap  
\- Page 44 : Initial CC clusters are only found in loanwords, and even
there they are rare. Con-  
sider the following:  
tr (2) trìkó ‘undershirt’ (\<French tricot) (=\> entre parenthèse ça
doit être la fréquence)  
Medial geminated CC  
Section 3.1.3 discussed licit geminates in native Tommo So stems, all of
which are sonorants. However, other geminates have entered the language
through loanwords, reduplication, ideophones, affixation, and vowel
syncope. The list below gives frequencies for all geminates in the
language. Geminate nasals, particularly /nn/ and /mm/, are extremely
common, and the frequency of each has been given as 50+. Geminate velar
nasals are unattested.  
rr (1) sárrì ‘misfortune’ (alternate pronunciation sárdì)  
\- Page 46 : yr (1) Kɔ́ yrá jìmL  
rp (2) dárpòy  
rt (2) màrtó  
rk (7) bárkè  
rs (4) sàrsám  
rd (8) kàrdándè  
rg (6) árgállà  
rj (1) kùrjà\~kùrjá  
rm (2) sàndàrmá  
rn (3+) gír-nɛ́  
\- Page 48 : Medial CCC  
If medial CC clusters are rare, then medial CCC clusters are practically
non-existent, as these clusters are usually broken up by epenthesis.
However, the lexicon contains three French loanwords with unrepaired CCC
clusters. These are as follows:  
àŋgró ‘wholesale’ (\<French en gros)  
Final CC  
Like medial CCC clusters, final CC clusters are extremely rare. There
are only four  
known cases in the lexicon:  
mɛ́ tr ‘meter’ (\<French mètre)  
Most of the time, these final clusters will be repaired with epenthetic
vowels, such as mɛ́turu for mɛ́tr, but occasionally speakers will
pronounce the cluster with no repair.

Glottologue :  
<https://glottolog.org/resource/languoid/id/tomm1242>

\- Page 27 : There are a few phonemes that are borrowed from Spanish,
including /d/, /g/, /ʎ/ and /r/, although the language had the two
prenasalized stops previously.  
\- Page 41 : Liquids l, (r)  
\- Page 44 : Liquids  
There are a few sounds (namely liquids) that seem to be phonemic because
they are not predictable, but they are extremely rare: the palatal
lateral approximant /ʎ/, the alveolar lateral approximant /l/, and the
alveolar tap /r/. The tap is certainly borrowed from Spanish (see
2.2.2.7), and the two lateral approximants may be as well.  
\- Page 45 : /r/  
ral ‘money’ (from Spanish real)  
rat ‘short amount of time’ (from Spanish rato)  
\- Page 47 : /r/ and /ʎ/ are also common and found only in loan words
(from Spanish or Inga).

Glottologue :  
<https://glottolog.org/resource/languoid/id/bora1263>

Thiesen, Wesley and David Weber. 2012. A Grammar of Bora with Special
Attention to Tone. (SIL International Publications in Linguistics, 148.)
Dallas, Texas: SIL International. xxxiii+550pp.  
\- Page 27 : r for /ɾ/  
y for either /j/ (the palatalized counterpart of /ɾ/) or the
palatalization of a preceding consonant.  
\- Page 33 : res plain ɾ (r)  
pal j^b (y)  
^b Or possibly ɾ^j.  
\- Page 35 : /ɾ/ (orthographic r) is a voiced alveolar flap. The
corresponding palatalized form is the palatal approximant \[j\]
(orthographic y). It occurs in the environment of /i/ (but never of
/ɨ/); for example:  
(31) a. mìjímíjì (miyímíyi) ‘eyes half closed’  
b. βìjíːβjɛ̀ (viyíívye) ‘to rotate’  
c. a ʧìʔíjò (llihíyo) ‘father’  
d. ɾoːʔò ∼ jòːʔò (roóho) ‘mole cricket’  
\[j\] may also be a phoneme in its own right; in the words of 32 it
occurs despite not being in an environment in which /ɾ/ is
palatalized:  
(32) a. ájánɛ́ kpɯ̀ (áyánéwu) ‘a little’  
b. jóːʔìí (yóóhií) ‘type of parrot’  
In the vast majority of cases, however, orthographic y represents either
(1) a palatalized /ɾ/ or (2) the palatalization of a preceding consonant
\[C^j\], as discussed in section 2.4.2.  
\- Page 39 : /ɾ/ becomes j (y) only after /i/, never after the /a/s that
are reconstructable as /\*ai/. Example 43 involves the suffix -ɾɛ̀ (-re)
‘only’:  
(43) a. òó-ɾɛ̀ (oóre) ‘only I’  
b. ìí-jɛ̀ (iíye) ‘only he himself’  
Example 44 involves the suffix -ɾà (-ra) ‘frustrative,
contraexpectation’:  
(44) a. ó máxʧhò-ɾá-ʔì (Ó májchoráhi.) ‘I ate, but not well.’  
I eat-frs-⟨t⟩  
b. ó máxʧhó-ì-já-ʔì (Ó májchóiyáhi.) ‘I would like to eat,  
I eat-fut-frs-⟨t⟩ but can’t.’

Autres sources :  
<https://clics.clld.org/languages/hubercolumbian-Bora>

La letra r es en la mayoría de las palabras la misma r del español, como
en la palabra ‘para’. Cuando está precedida o seguida de una vocal
nasalizada, la r suena más como la n. También se encuentran a individuos
que la pronuncian como l en ciertos contextos.

2 inventaires  
\- UPSID ɾ pas d’allophones  
\- SAPHON ɾ pas d’allophones

Caudmont, J. 1954. Fonologia del Guambiano. Revista Colombiana de
Antropologia 5. 189–206.  
\- Page 193 : /r R/  
\- Page 194 : Apicales r R  
/r/ kere ‘aji’  
/R/ pareRi ‘sacerdote’  
\- Page 196 : /r/ dental, apical  
/R/ palatal, apical  
\- Page 197 : Practicamente reducidas a /m, n, l/ porque la asimilacion
no se realiza con /r/  
\- Page 198 : En la agrupacion /tR/ la oclusiva /t/ se articula siempre
sorda:  
tRolmera ‘frutas’  
\- Page 199 : /r/ se pronuncia siempre vibrante apical sonora, ej.:  
pura ‘maiz’  
En posicion inicial de palabra la oposicion r/l se neutraliza y solo se
encuentra la /l/, hay, sin embargo, algunas pocas excepciones entre las
palabras prestadas del castellano, ej.: rinyomera ‘rinones’  
/R/ se pronuncia fricativa apical mediopalatal, indiferentemente sonora
o osrda. En la agrupacion /tR/ se pronuncia sorda por asimilacion de la
/t/, ej.:  
pareRi ‘sacerdote’  
tRik ‘labio’  
tRokol ‘dentadure’  
Aqui tambien, en posicion inicial de palabra la oposicion R/l desaparece
y solamente aparece la /l/, con algunas exceptiones en los prestamos
espagnoles, ej.:  
Rayo ‘rayo’ \[ržayo\]  
\- Page 201 : Los 12 fonemas consonanticos se encuentran el principio de
la silaba con algunas restricciones en las silabas inicicales de las
palabras donde occuren donde ocurren varios casos de neutralizacion
(l/r/R). Encontraremos, pues, en la inicial de palabra las siguientes
consonantes /\[consonants\]/ y al principio de una silaba en el interior
de una palabra, las mismas consonantes y ademas /r/ y /R/, ej.:  
/r/ kere ‘aji’  
/R/ lusuRi ‘flauta’  
\- Page 202 : En posicion final de silaba solamente se encuentran las
siguientes consonantes sencillas /r/  
/r/ terté ‘claridad’  
Grupas consonanticos explosivos:  
/tR-/ tRik ‘labios’ \[tRig\] o \[tRige\]  
/tr-/ trago ‘trago’  
\- Page 203 : /-rš-/

Branks, T. and Branks, J. 1973. Fonologia del Guambiano. (Sistemas
fonologicos de idiomas colombianos, 2.) Loma Linda, Colombia: Summer
Institute of Linguistcs.  
\- Page 41 : Vibrante r  
\- Page 42 : /r/ pa’ran ‘falto’  
\- Pahe 44 : En la sigiente seccion descriptiva las clases funcionales
estan usadas para mostrar en resumen la compleja pertubacion
consonantica que ocurre en las posiciones C.C a través de los limites
silabicos. Algunos de los ejemplos usados para ilustrar las
perturbaciones C.C estan senalados con dos acentos porque en realidad
son dos palabras fonologicas igualmente acentualdas, que componen una
frase fonologica, aunque se escribe aqui como una palabra. \[…\]
mientras /č./ se incluye en la clase Ž con /r/ y /ž/. Aunque la /r/ y
la /ž/ no comparten toda la distibucion de /č./, porque la /r/ no
ocurre en inicial de silaba, en donde tienen distribucion parecida
tienen el mismo tipo de perturbacion. Por eso la clase Ž (/r/, /ž/,
/č./)puede ser considerada mas como agrupacion con proposito
descriptivo que como un tipo de clase solido como el que representa S o
N.  
\- Page 47 : /r/ La vibrante simple alveolar sonora \[ř\] occurre en
posicion intervocalica y en los modelas Ž.g(barré), \[etc.\]  
La vibrante multiple alveolar sorda \[r̃\]occure al final de enunciado y
en los modelos Š \[etc.\]

Branks, Thomas and Branks, Judith. 1973. Sistemas Fonol’ogicos de
Idiomas Colombianos. (2.) Ministerio de Gobierno. 39–56pp. -\> Pas
cherché mais ça doit être la même référence que précédement

Vásquez, Beatriz. 2000. Guambiano: algunos aspectos sobre morfolog’ia
nominal. In de Pérez, María Stella González and de Montes, María Luisa
Rodríguez (eds.), Lenguas ind’igenas de Colombia: Una visi’on
descriptiva, 155–156. -\> Pas cherché

Long, Violeta. 1985. Estudio de la lengua guambiano. In Longacre, Robert
E. (ed.), University of St. Andrews, Centre for Latin American Studies,
Working Paper. -\> Pas cherché

<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coconucan_language>

Granadillo, Tania. 2006. An ethnographic account of language

\- Page 73 : Kurripako consonants are presented in Table 5 in IPA
characters with the exception of the retroflex trill for which there is
no character in IPA.  
Tap P  
Preaspirated Tap ʰP  
Trill (retroflex) ɽ  
Most consonants have an aspirated counterpart, the aspirated counterpart
to the trill is the retroflex fricative, and the counterpart to the
bilabial approximant is the bilabial fricative.

voiceless ɺ̥

Glottologue :  
<https://glottolog.org/resource/languoid/id/curr1243>

de Souza, Erick Marcelo Lima. 2012. Estudo fonológico da língua
Baniwa-Kuripako. (MA thesis, Universidade Estadual de Campinas;
338pp.)  
\- Page 37 : Para as consoantes, Taylor (1991) apresenta o seguinte:  
r hr rr hrr  
\- Page 38 : r soante vibrante alveolar lateral oral  
hr r pré-aspirada  
rr soante vibrante palatal oral  
hrr rr pré-aspirada  
\- Page 40 : Valadares (1993) apresenta uma proposta de quadro
fonológico para as vogais e para as consoantes do Kuripako  
ɹ ɻ  
\- Page 42 : Ramirez (2001)  
flapes laterais ɺ  
aproximantes ɻ  
\- Page 44 : Granadillo (2006)  
Tap P  
Preaspirated Tap ʰP  
Trill (retroflex) ɽ  
\- Page 78 : Flepe lateral em Kuripako  
a) Alveolar sonoro – \[ɺ\] como em \[ɺi.wa.’dzo.ɺe\] ‘brasa’  
b) Alveolar ensurdecido – \[ɺ̥\] como em \[ɺ̥je.’ta.hə\] ‘aquele’  
Este fone tem oscilação, em ambas as variantes, entre um tepe alveolar e
um tepe lateral, de fato algumas imagens de espectograma parecem nos
indicar um tepe alveolar, contudo os falantes Baniwa diziam que para
reproduzir bem o som é necessário produzir um fone entre um R e um L do
português, isto é, um tepe alveolar e uma líquida simultaneamente.
Assim, representaremos, para fins de escolha entre um símbolo, em nossas
transcrições fonológicas o tepe lateral /ɺ/.  
\- Page 81 : flepe laterais ɺ̥ ɺ  
\- Page 113 : O fonema /ɺ/  
O fonema /ɺ/ realiza-se como flepe lateral alveolar sonoro \[ɺ\] ou
próximo de um tepe alveolar \[ɾ\]. (49) traz exemplos de Baniwa e (50)
de Kuripako.  
(50) /ɺ/ em Kuripako  
a)\[’ɺi.tʊ\]  
/‘ɺi.tu/  
3SgM-defecar  
‘ele defeca’  
\[examples\]  
\- Page 115 : A realização fonética deste fonema é muito oscilante entre
um tepe lateral ou alveolar, contudo mantivemos a representação fonética
de tepe lateral, dadas as orientações do falante Baniwa de que para
realizar este som é necessário produzir algo entre um R e um L
português. O fone flepe lateral sonoro \[ɺ\] ocorre em diversos
contextos possíveis na língua, em início de palavra como aclive
silábico, tanto no início quanto no fim de palavra, nunca ocorrendo em
coda. Porém não há ocorrências de palavras em que \[ɺ\] seja antecedido
de /e/ ou /i/. O que é indício de uma oposição neutralizável entre /ɺ/ e
/ʐ/ nesse contexto.  
Este fonema pode relizar-se como \[ɺ\], ou \[ʂ\] diante de /h/, este
último antecedido de /i/, /i:/, /e/ ou /e:/ como veremos em 4.3.2.2.

Autres sources :  
<https://clics.clld.org/languages/hubercolumbian-Curripaco>  
Recherches : r -\> 62 entries (filtered from 344 total entries)  
(=\> On a un ɾ et des r dans Form is source alors que dans CLICS form ce
ne sont que des r)

\- Page 23 : Flap r  
The flap is voiced retroflexed dental, /r/ \[z̆^{ř.}\] \[ř.^{z̆}\]
\[č.\] \[^{z̆}ř.\] \[ř.\]. Voiced alveopalatal sibilant with
retroflexed dental off-glide \[z̆^{ř.}\] occurs word initial, and also
word medial when /r/ occurs initially. Voiced retroflexed dental flap
with alveopalatal sibilant off-glide \[ř.^{z̆}\] occurs word final.
Voiceless retroflexed alveopalatal stop \[č.\] varies freely with
voiced retroflexed dental flap \[ř.\] preceding voiceless stops. Voiced
retroflexed dental flap with or without alveopalatal sibilant on-glide
\[^{z̆}ř.\] \~ \[ř.\] occurs inter-vocalically.  
/ruraku/ \[z̆<sup>{ř.}ʋz̆</sup>{ř.}ako\] ‘he is making’  
/yawar/ \[yawař.^{z̆}\] ‘blood’  
/surkuku/ \[suč.kʋkʋ\] \~ \[suř.kʋkʋ\] ‘he is taking out’  
/kara/ \[ka^{z̆}ř.a\] \~ \[kař.a\] ‘he gave’  
/wamra/ \[’wam^{b}ř.a\] ‘child’  
\- Page 26 : In open syllables or syllables closed by /r/, when
preceding the nucleus of the stress group, there is free variation
ranging between \[ʋ\] and \[ɔ\],  
\- Page 28 : Syllabic consonant allophones  
Flaps  
\[ř.^{ə}\]occurs preceding C\_vd  
\[l̩̆\] occurs preceding all consonants/  
/warmi/ \[wař.^{ə}.me\] ‘woman’  
/alku/ \[al̩̆ko\] ‘dog’
